URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS97038938	2020-06-18 10:57:55	6996	species	Neotropic Cormorant	Phalacrocorax brasilianus			1				United States	US	Nevada	US-NV	Clark	US-NV-003		33			Las Vegas--Sunset Park	L456488	H	36.0685619	-115.1126862	1998-03-01		obsr121218	S6778453	Incidental	P20	EBIRD					0		0	1	1			Submitted by: Jim Eidel;  Found by: Bob Flores	
URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS162155191	2020-06-18 09:51:47	6996	species	Neotropic Cormorant	Phalacrocorax brasilianus			1				United States	US	Nevada	US-NV	Clark	US-NV-003		33			Las Vegas--Floyd Lamb Park at Tule Springs	L139900	H	36.3249025	-115.2665940	2012-08-27	07:15:00	obsr209998	S11458583	Incidental	P20	EBIRD				1	0		0	1	1			This bird was found yesterday by Andrew Lee.  I left my place in Sparks last night at about 9 PM and drove all night (with a few neccesary safety naps) down to Las Vegas.  When I arrived a little after 7 AM, the bird was swimming with five Double-crested Cormorants.  The D-C Cormorants were bullying the Neo and would nip at its tail and give it short chases as they swam around in a rather tight group.  The Neo didn't seem to mind the bullying and stayed close to them.  The birds seemed restless and flew for no apparent reason.  They circled around a couple times and landed in a dead topped tree at the southeast corner of Tule Lake.  The birds only stayed a minute before taking off again and flying in an even larger circle roughly encoumpassing the entire park propery limits.  They returned and landed in the dead tree once again.  I was able to get very nice photos of the bird in the water, in flight and in the tree.  The bird seemed agitated when it was in the tree and was attempting to tear off a broken branch that was hanging by some strands of fibrous bark.  After determining it didn't like it's perch, possibly due to the nearby hanging broken branch, it moved further up a nearby branch.  It then began to preen.  I photographed it and watched it for about 30 minutes before wandering off.  The birds diminutive size was very evident in the presence of the Double-crests.  The long tail (relating to the overall body size), yellowish face and gular area and the thin, albeit indistinct, pale to whitish boarder to the feathers ajacent to the bare parts of the face near the gape were all evident.  Also the dark loral region was obvious.  I missed this species by a single day 14 years ago in Nevada, so this sighting was particularily rewarding to me.

First observed swimming and feeding in the center of the first pond with 15 or so Common Mergansers and a couple of Double-crested Cormorants. What first called our attention to this bird was that it was smaller than the other DCC and about the same size as the CM. Upon closer examination using 10x42 field glasses slight differences were apparent in the face and bill between this bird and the DCCs--these differences included a whitish area between the bill and face and an overall smaller less brightly colored bill. Talking with several other birders in the area we were advised that this bird has been in the park area for several months.
